B007O3QC8K|5.0|Something that continues to astound me is the falling off of the number of recordings that are coming out of Berlin nowadays.  Long gone are the days of Karajan who was a recording master, and even Abbado managed a respectable number of discs, including some of the best Mahler ever committed to tape.  However, it's 2012 now and we must make do with what we have, which brings us to this particular recording.<br />  Having read a biography of Simon Rattle, I'm surprised to find that he's long been a champion of Bruckner's 9th symphony - this coming from a man who has done magical things with the music of Schoenberg, Bartok, etc.  So why tackle Bruckner, a bastion of the Romantic era? (To the best of my knowledge, there are only two other recordings of Sir Simon and Bruckner - a 7th from Birmingham and a wonderful 4th from Berlin.)  Well, I'll offer up a couple of theories.  Firstly, Bruckner is a composer that Karajan long championed, and his Bruckner recordings with Berlin are astounding, especially the two recordings of the 9th he made.  They are powerful, well controlled readings.  Bruckner can't be accused of being a good structuralist, but Karajan held these baggy monsters together and made them into something really special.  I think Sir Simon wants to show that Berlin is still an excellent Bruckner orchestra.  I'm happy to report that this is very much a fact.  Secondly, Bruckner's harmonies in this symphony are more looking forwards to the Second Viennese School rather than backwards towards say, Wagner.  Who better to bring out these qualities than the conductor who has probably done more for twentieth century music in the last couple of decades than anyone else on the international circuit?  Out of the available recordings of the 9th, this one does that the most.  No fuddy-duddy old cathedral Bruckner here - this is a fresh interpretation.  Rattle has a tendency for fussiness, but I find that to be mostly absent here.  Granted it's not as sweeping as Karajan but it moves with power that is inherent in this music.  The scherzo is punchy in the loud parts, lyrical in the slow parts.  I find this to be a good way to approach Bruckner scherzos because well, that's how they are.  No one can touch Karajan in the adagio in this work, but that is a summit that no one will ever likely be able to scale.  But Rattle isn't trying to take on Karajan, and what we get fits in with the rest of his vision.  Rattle tries to show us the connections between the movements, and the result is a line that goes from the beginning of the symphony to the end, which brings me to my next point.<br />It's not easy to guess what a composer would have done, but there have been some very good attempts over the years, such as the work done with Mahler's 10th, Elgar's 3rd, and so on.  Out of all the work done with matters of this nature, this realization of the finale to Bruckner's 9th is very faithful to the composer.  We are still very much in the realm of Bruckner here.  There's no attempt to change the way in which Bruckner wrote.  All of the trademarks are here - the alternation between massive brass chorales, lyrical melodies in the strings, a powerful coda - there's no mistake that anyone other than Anton Bruckner wrote this.  It also gives Simon Rattle a chance to make a permanent stamp in the Bruckner pantheon. After all, this music hasn't been heard before.  Rattle and the orchestra play with the utmost commitment to Bruckner's fleshed out vision.  I can't find much to criticize musically with this finale.  It looks back to the first movement for some inspiration, but I won't call this a bad thing.<br />  Technically, all of the aspects one could hope for are there.  The Berliners play with their usual superhuman virtuosity.  I cannot reiterate enough how fortunate we are to have these musicians in our midst.  Sonically, this is about as good as it gets in the Philharmonie.  Not even in the heyday of Karajan were the sonics ever world class, but every section is well recorded.  The brass in particular are hefty in tone, and this is important in Bruckner.  I hope one day that EMI can get decent sonics out of this problematic hall. All in all, we should be very grateful for this recording.  One can only hope for a return to a time when recordings streamed out of Berlin with regularity.ut every section is well recorded.  The brass in particular are hefty in tone, and this is important in Bruckner.  I hope one day that EMI can get decent sonics out of this problematic hall. All in all, we should be very grateful for this recording.  One can only hope for a return to a time when recordings streamed out of Berlin with regularity.

There is no such thing as a true and correct interpretation and so one must make up one's own mind.  For me it was like my first experience of Glenn Gould playing Bach - it just seemed so right.  Certainly Boulez creates the best of a certain type of interpretation, and I don't think anyone can match the kind of balance and elegance Boulez gets from his orchestras.  For these composers.  FYI - I think his own music is terrible and an evil influence on modern music.  But I am very glad he gets to direct orchestras. A romantic approach for both composers is just not right.  It feels overdone because the romance is already built in to the music. That aspect of the story had actual potential, but was inconsistent in their respective desires as subconscious characters.<br /><br />And the Adonis demi-god that Christian is? Get real...He's 26, has a jet, a helicopter, plays perfect piano, is a wine enthusiast, is a billionaire, comes from a perfect family, is a philanthropist, and his *one* negative is actually a positive because it causes Ana to \\"flush\\" every other page? I'm sorry, I know books are supposed to take us away from reality, but it also has to connect with the reader in some way -- no one, fictional or not -- is that perfect. And why, exactly, does society insist that stalking is romantic? I find Christian's abilities to stalk Ana quite creepy.<br /><br />The sex is dull, albeit decent for some who haven't read true erotica before. You'd do better finding one that didn't originate as some adolescent desire for Edward Cullen to tie her down and flog her.

B000002GBD|5.0|Nazareth's HAIR OF THE DOG is one of the most outstanding albums in the annals of rock/metal. It ranks up there with PARNOID, ZEPPELIN II, APPETITE FOR DESTRUCTION, BALLS TO THE WALL, BACK IN BLACK, SCREAMING FOR VENGEANCE, RIDE THE LIGHTNING, SYMBOLIC, ACT III, TURN LOOSE THE SWANS, THE OATH, THE MANTLE, THE GODDESS, OMEGA WAVE, NOSFERATU, and the like.<br /><br />Although Nazareth were never progressive in the typical sense, they tended to experiment a lot from album to album and therefore refused to be categorized. Were they rock, metal, pop, blues or what? On HAIR OF THE DOG they chose the metal route and this is one kick-axx metal CD. A lot of Nazareth's 70s and 80s material sounds dated to my ears (although they have a lot of worthy material), but not HAIR OF THE DOG. The album may have been released in 1975, but it's a timeless metal/rock recording, pure and simple.<br /><br />Of course everyone knows the radio staples \\"Hair of the Dog\\" and \\"Love Hurts.\\" The former is both heavy and ultra-catchy with Dan McCafferty's patented gravelly vocals. Even my mother (R.I.P.), who could never warm up to anything heavier than the Beetles, confessed to liking the song and expressed how catchy it was! \\"Love Hurts\\" was a modernization of The Everly Brothers' ballad and the band really made it their own.<br /><br />\\"Miss Misery\\" is a Sabbath-inspired doomy masterpiece as Dan laments a witchy woman he foolishly got involved with. Everything meshes together for a brilliant classic piece - the vocals, the lyrics, the guitars and drums. Although the rhythm guitar is ultra-simple, sometimes \\"simplicity is the composer's greatest tool.\\"<br /><br />\\"Please Don't Judas Me\\" is even doomier, but not in the heavy-chord sense. It's kind of gothic with a semi-chanted chorus and marching beat. The lyrics are strong as Dan beseeches the listener to not do him wrong.<br /><br />\\"Changing Times\\" is another highlight with a more driving Zeppelin-esque tempo.<br /><br />\\"Beggars Day\\" is more rock than metal, but Dan's raspy vocals break it into the realm of metal. Brian Johnson obviously took lessons from McCafferty (not to mention Accept's Udo and even Axl Rose to some degree). \\"Rose in the Heather\\" is a sweet instrumental coda, melodically `acoustic' with a lead guitar.<br /><br />\\"Whiskey Drinkin' Woman\\" is a cool Lynyrd Skynyrd-esque number.<br /><br />The mellow ballad \\"Guilty\\" was replaced by \\"Love Hurts\\" for the USA release. Needless to say, \\"Love Hurts\\" is vastly superior and it was a good decision to switch them.<br /><br />The name of the album and title song - \\"Hair of the Dog\\" - was always a mystery to me until I discovered that it was originally intended to be \\"Heir of the Dog,\\" i.e. another way of saying `son of a biyatch,' which, of course, is the phrase used in the unforgettable chorus.<br /><br />Although the band's name harkens from Nazareth, PA, of all places, it naturally evokes the home town of Jesus Christ in Israel. Interestingly, the incredible cover art is a stylized depiction of the multi-headed \\"beast\\" that comes out of the sea, as depicted in Revelation 13:1-2.<br /><br />Needless to say, HAIR OF THE DOG was Nazareth's magnum opus; everything magically came together on this outstanding, potent work. Never to be repeated; it stands alone in the Nazareth catalog. It's a must-have for any rock/metal listener.<br /><br />GRADE: A+rock than metal, but Dan's raspy vocals break it into the realm of metal. Brian Johnson obviously took lessons from McCafferty (not to mention Accept's Udo and even Axl Rose to some degree). \\"Rose in the Heather\\" is a sweet instrumental coda, melodically `acoustic' with a lead guitar.<br /><br />\\"Whiskey Drinkin' Woman\\" is a cool Lynyrd Skynyrd-esque number.<br /><br />The mellow ballad \\"Guilty\\" was replaced by \\"Love Hurts\\" for the USA release. 0385344228|4.0|Is this novel a courtroom drama, a psychological study of a family, an introspective study of a man, or is it about truth and justice?  Or all of the above?  It's hard to tell in this rambling book which attempts to keep the reader in suspense and leaves much to the imagination.<br /><br />Andy Barber, the First Assistant DA in Newton, MA, is thee man who faces the questions posed by the story and really doesn't come to grips with the essential problems raised.  His 14-year-old son is accused of murdering a fellow student and goes to trial for Murder One.  Did he or didn't he?  Andy, who initially ran the original investigation, does not believe his son is capable of doing the deed.  The effect of the pressures of the trial on Andy and his wife are weakly described.  The courtroom drama is, to some extent, extremely well done, but, for the most part, drawn out to a great degree.  And the snideness of the comments about Andy's replacement when he's taken off the case and during the trial are too often petty.<br /><br />On the whole, the novel is an interesting presentation, but could have been edited severely, especially the front end which drags on slowly until the book picks up steam toward the middle.  It is no spoiler to note that there is more than one surprise waiting for the reader at the end, some attention-grabbing, others a little far-fetched.  That said, it is an off-beat novel that is recommended.

B000ZELISO|3.0|J.K. Rowling's tale is, as always, rife with fresh ideas, and her believable characters continue to endure further challenges in the process of growing-up.  Apart from the story's more fanciful elements, there are also some real-life lessons being learned here (in boy-girl relationships and academic cheating among them).<br /><br />That said, Rowling's extensive narrative doesn't fit so comfortably into a two-and-a-half hour running time.  Screenwriter Steve Kloves manages to get most of it all to work within the prescribed length (though it is hard to see the point of invented scenes like Harry's flirtation with a muggle girl in the London tube station when that screen-time could have been alloted to more of Rowling's own ideas).<br /><br />Director David Yates handles the material adroitly, but his style feels more perfunctory than the series' previous directors.  One scene in particular (which I won't spoil), is a major -- and devastating -- turning point in Rowling's tale, but it lacks any dramatic intensity and proves something of a lead balloon.  The film is technically well-photographed (by Bruno Delbonnel) but is awash in low-key, shadowy lighting, and a generally drab, washed-out colour scheme.  While effective in the spookier moments, this look doesn't suit the scenes of intimacy or humor, and tends to cast a dreary pall about the film.  Nicholas Hooper provides a serviceable score, but it too is lacklustre compared to the symphonic grandeur of John Williams' Harry Potter music.<br /><br />However the performers are (as usual) first rate. The established cast bring ever-more freshness to their parts, while newcomer Hero Fiennes-Tiffin (nephew of Ralph Fiennes) makes for an unnervingly sinister Tom Riddle, and veteran actor Jim Broadbent makes a fine Professor Slughorn.<br /><br />Harry Potter and the Half-Blood Prince is a wonderful story which results in a very good film.  Warner Bros. Blu-ray disc boasts an excellent transfer with crisp detail.  But it does lack the epic sweep of the earlier films and doesn't deliver a satisfying emotional payoff, and so proves one of the less-enjoyable Potter adaptations.

0385504209|3.0|In The Da Vinci Code,  Dan Brown weaves a complex tale involving the Catholic church, the French police and the supposed descendants of Jesus Christ. He claims that Jesus Christ eloped with Mary Magdalene and that their descendants and documents concerning  the church are carefully guarded by the Priory of Sion, a secret society that practices Goddess worship. His main characters, Robert Langdon, a Harvard symbologist reminiscent of a bookish Indiana Jones, and Sophie Nehru, a French codebreaker who struggles to break any of the code she encounters, are forced in a treasure hunt for the \\"Holy Grail\\" by the murder of her grandfather by the Catholic Church.<br /><br /> Dan Brown studied at Amherst college and Phillips Exeter Academy, where he grew fascinated with the inverse correlation between science and religion. He has written numerous books on the conflict between secret societies and the modern world.<br /><br /> The author supports his controversial thesis with historical evidence relating to the council of Nicaea and the adoption of Christianity by the Roman Emperor Constantine. He also references scholars who support his claims through Langdon's thoughts. In writing the novel, Brown claims to have extensively research the topics and even interviewed members of organizations central to his plot.<br /><br /> One slightly frustrating detail of Browns deciphering of ancient art and architecture is that he is simply not a good enough writer to describe the pieces, and he does not include images of the works his novel deals so extensively with. Although Brown claims to have researched the topics he wrote about, there are numerous historical inaccuracies that both undermine his central claim and reduce his credibility as a writer. The Priory of Sion he writes about was proved to be an elaborate hoax developed by Pierre Plantard in the 1990's. Brown also misinterprets, or simply bends Gnostic beliefs about the divinity of Jesus into the basis of a Jesus Mary love affair. Brown has responded to these claims by not responding; he has continued to state that all of the history art and architecture listed in the novel is completely true, even when faced with overwhelming evidence that it is not.<br /><br /> The books argument is not convincing, because the author uses historical inaccuracies and goes in circles too much to make a definitive case for his argument.  A quote on the Priory of Sion in the book, \\"They call themselves the Prieur&eacute; de Sion-the Priory of Sion. They're based here in France and attract powerful members from all over Europe. In fact, they are one of the oldest surviving secret societies on earth.\\"( Brown 113). At a Priory of Sion history website, \\" after asking Plantard to swear on Oath - Plantard admitted that he made everything up; whereupon Plantard was given a serious warning and advised not to \\"play games\\" with the French Judicial System.\\" ([...]). Dan Brown is contradicted by many sources and indeed the French government. This carelessness in composing a literary work casts doubt on the novel as a whole and debunks his overall claim.<br /><br /> This book different than any other historical fiction book I have read because rather than taking place in a different time period, it takes place in the modern day and references the historical past. It also deals with a very controversial topic, which is common among bestsellers as it instills curiosity in the reader.<br /><br /> This book would appeal to mystery readers or to people who love a good conspiracy theory, however I would not recommend this book to the general public because it is in general a badly written and badly referenced publicity stunt.to these claims by not responding; he has continued to state that all of the history art and architecture listed in the novel is completely true, even when faced with overwhelming evidence that it is not. 0140364536|5.0|For our family's summer reading selection, I picked the mammoth four-hundred-page Watership Down by Richard Adams. Some novels will feel like an endurance test just four pages in. Without fail, whenever I read Watership Down, those four hundred pages feel to me like a summer holiday. My husband, who groaned at the idea of reading such a long book about rabbits, instead has eagerly returned to the book whenever he has a spare minute.<br /><br />\\"The rabbits arrived at their new home - but I'm not even halfway through the book,\\" my husband observed. \\"What in the world can happen next?\\" Richard Adams never ceases to amaze me with how he handles pacing. On the surface, his rabbit tale is a fairly simple one: rabbits must escape their doomed warren and find a new home. In less capable hands, Watership Down would have been half as long and packed with chases, storms, brawls, and catastrophes. But Adams never hurries his tale. Often he stops to leisurely describe the scenery or to wax philosophical. Sometimes he even dedicates an entire chapter to a rabbit folktale. Yet these interludes rarely feel like interruptions; rather, these interesting descriptions, musings, and stories of the rabbit god Frith and the great trickster El-ahrairah increase the momentum. Adams also never resorts to implausible plot twists. Instead he is perfectly content to tell his simple tale and trust his readers to listen--and so we do.<br /><br />I am astounded at how imperfect and yet captivating are Adams' rabbits. Take Fiver, a runt who has been blessed with the gift of prophecy. Then there is Fiver's older brother, Hazel, whose greatest strength is his ability to identify and trust the strengths of others. By speaking with conviction of his trust in his brother's visions of doom, he convinces a small band of rabbits to leave the warren and seek a new home. Yet even he also makes errors in judgment. One day to prove his greatness as a leader, despite desperate warnings from Fiver, Hazel sets out to rescue pet rabbits from a nearby farm - and almost meets a violent end. Darker characters also exist. For example, let me introduce you to Strawberry who lives in a different warren. He hides a dark secret, which almost becomes the downfall of one of Hazel's companions. Despite his betrayal, he eventually joins Hazel's warren and becomes a great asset. And then there is Captain Holly. One of the powerful elite at the Sandleford warren, he attempts to arrest Hazel and his fellow rabbits to prevent them from leaving the warren. After failing to do so, he later seeks refuge at Hazel's new warren and becomes a valued contributor. These rabbits are fallible, allowing Adams to present many stirring moments of heartache and redemption.<br /><br />Adams considered the Berkshire countryside to also be a character in Watership Down. He certainly lavishes great attention to it. In a random survey which I conducted of twenty books for young people, over half of them dedicated their first paragraph to introducing character. And by character, I mean a human being or least an animal. Half of the books didn't refer to setting until the second paragraph or even the second page. And when they did, they often only dedicated one sentence to description. A particularly generous author may perhaps devote an entire early paragraph to setting. In contrast, Adams spends the first three paragraphs on the landscape. That's not to say Adams wastes time waxing poetic. In each paragraph, he details the scenery but also the place of the rabbits within it: \\"The May sunset was red in clouds and there was still half an hour to twilight. The dry slope was dotted with rabbits--some nibbling at their grass....\\" Those paragraphs might be long, but they effectively establish a tone of peace, which within a few pages is quietly interrupted. Readers are all the richer for how saturated in reality Watership Down really is.<br /><br />\\"No author today would think of writing a four-hundred-page book about rabbits,\\" my husband observed. Adams himself did not begin with such an audacious goal; Watership Down started out as story told by Adams to his daughters on car rides. As the years went on, his daughters encouraged him to share his story with the world, and finally he took the plunge and set the tale to paper. But Watership Down received so many rejections that Adams considered publishing it himself. Then a small-time publisher named Rex Collins accepted it for a two thousand copy run. Since that time it has rightfully sold millions of copies.ut rabbits,\\" my husband observed. As the years went on, his daughters encouraged him to share his story with the world, and finally he took the plunge and set the tale to paper. But Watership Down received so many rejections that Adams considered publishing it himself. Then a small-time publisher named Rex Collins accepted it for a two thousand copy run. Since that time it has rightfully sold millions of copies.

B007KDFWRG|5.0|Update: After listening to this CD set for 2 weeks, my initial very positive reaction is confirmed and strengthened. While some may not like the extended versions of the songs on this CD (many clock in at 10 minutes plus), I think they are superior to the studio versions. They capture the energy and the high level of musicianship that I heard when I saw the TTB live. The jamming and soloing that extends these performances always has purpose and direction, skillfully providing variation on the basic melodies and song structures. (And what would be the point of a live CD that simply duplicated the studio versions anyway?) All this plus the finest female voice in blues/rock make this one of my favorite CDs.<br /><br />About 6 or 7 years ago, I saw both the Susan Tedeschi Band and the Derek Trucks Band perform separately but on the same bill at a concert in Baltimore. ST & DT each came out for a song or two with the other's band, but it was two distinct bands performing. I remember thinking at the time that combining the two into a single band would be a great move. DTB's singer, who also appears on Everybody's Talkin', is very good, but there's something about Susan's powerful, female voice-- and the contrast it provides to the heavy and well-played blues/soul/funk of DT & company-- that takes things to the next level.<br /><br />Everybody's Talkin' is a terrific concert CD. It mixes extended versions of songs from their studio album, Revelator, with covers of classics. While I tend to be wary of such efforts and the tendency of extended jams to dissolve into aimless noodling, that is generally not the case here as this band has the chops to pull it off. The result is something that is both familiar and different from what Revelator (a very good CD in its own right) has to offer. Highly recommended.

This filming gimmick gets in the way of the story since every scene has to invent a reason why somebody is filming it.
